  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a97663e6-72f2-4c65-945e-f452b3e37429'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer13273581644662290292">وقعت أحداث هذه الرواية في حي خان الخليلي فأخذت الرواية اسمها منه. تنتقل العائلة الصغيرة المكونة من أم وأب وأخوين إلى هذا الحي طلبًا للأمان. فنرى في هذه الرواية الشباب وهو يافعاً يانعاً في شخص "رشدي" الأخ الأصغر ونراه مستأنساً حين يقع في شباك الحب ونراه يذوى مما قدر له حتى يختفي ويوارى طي الذكريات.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1291062795" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14882192716484141660">Rating: 3.5/4.0<br/><br/>I loved how the author described the village and how he made all the characters very human. Despite the book having a simple story, the author's underlying message was that there is nothing perfect. Nobody is perfect and no place is perfect. The story is set in the 1940s, in the world war and it tells the story of a family who moves from their neighborhood to another neighborhood named Khan Al Khalili (Title of the book) for safety purposes. Two brothers fall in love with the same</span>
  <span id="freeText14882192716484141660" style="display:none">Rating: 3.5/4.0<br /><br />I loved how the author described the village and how he made all the characters very human. Despite the book having a simple story, the author's underlying message was that there is nothing perfect. Nobody is perfect and no place is perfect. The story is set in the 1940s, in the world war and it tells the story of a family who moves from their neighborhood to another neighborhood named Khan Al Khalili (Title of the book) for safety purposes. Two brothers fall in love with the same girl. Who is going to get the girl? You have read to know this. But don't get it wrong. Although love is a big part of the book but it is not all what the book is about. I would call the story more relating to humanity and human nature in different situations than just being about love.<br /><br />I would recommend you reading this. Nice reading.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_44946724" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44946724" />
    <a title="Cameron" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1245760-cameron"><img alt="Cameron" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44946724?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cameron" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cameron" href="/user/show/1245760-cameron">Cameron</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789774161919" href="/book/show/4900242-khan-al-khalili">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44946724" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3382911568453002789">In a 1992 interview with The Paris Review, Egyptian writer Naguib Mafhouz, author of The Cairo Trilogy and winner of the 1988 Nobel Prize for Literature, acknowledged the absence of heroes from his stories, saying he liked to create recognizable characters from everyday life. <br/><br/>Why? Because I look at our society with a critical eye and find nothing extraordinary in the people I see.<br/><br/>Some might call that pessimistic, while others might call it realistic. However, given his compassionate warts-and-al</span>
  <span id="freeText3382911568453002789" style="display:none">In a 1992 interview with The Paris Review, Egyptian writer Naguib Mafhouz, author of The Cairo Trilogy and winner of the 1988 Nobel Prize for Literature, acknowledged the absence of heroes from his stories, saying he liked to create recognizable characters from everyday life. <br /><br />Why? Because I look at our society with a critical eye and find nothing extraordinary in the people I see.<br /><br />Some might call that pessimistic, while others might call it realistic. However, given his compassionate warts-and-all treatment of men and women alike, a third word comes to mind: humane.<br /><br />Mahfouz, who died in 2006, rose to fame in Egypt in the late 1950s with the publication of The Cairo Trilogy -- Palace Walk, Palace of Desire and Sugar Street -- the multi-generational saga of Al-Sayyid Ahmad and his family that spans the two world wars and runs to more than 1,200 pages. At home, Al-Sayyid Ahmad is a stern, tradition-minded taskmaster, appalled that his beautiful daughter might steal glances at handsome men passing below the family balcony, while outside the home he is a jovial lover of wit, wine and women, a side he never shows to his family. He’s a hypocrite, yet believes he’s doing right by his family with his do-as-I-say-not-as-I-do approach. His massive personality affects family and friends in myriad ways; his influence both bad and good throughout the trilogy.<br /><br />Fans of this monumental work will savor Khan al-Khalili, another work of social realism, first published in Arabic in 1946 and now translated into English for the first time.<br /><br />The novel takes place in Cairo during World War II. Just before Ramadan, 1941, the Axis Powers are bombing the al-Sakakini quarter of the city, so Ahmad Akif, a 40-year-old bachelor who lives with his parents and works at the post office in a dead-end job, uproots the family to the ancient quarter of Khan al-Khalili, hopeful the Germans will spare the historic neighborhood famous for its bazaar and the al-Husayn Mosque, named in honor of Mohammad’s grandson. <br /><br />The Akifs, we quickly discern, are not heroic people. The father was forced to retire from the government twenty years earlier because of insolent behavior. Consequently, support of the family fell to Ahmad, who never finished his studies and won’t be free of his obligation to the family until his younger brother, Rushdi, earns his degree. Ahmad loves his family (surprisingly, he doesn’t hold a grudge against his father) and yet he harbors the delusion that his genius (for something; anything) will never be recognized. He is “a martyr to injustice, a genius consigned early to the grave, a victim of malicious fate.” That, too, is why he’s never found a wife, he tells himself.<br /><br />All these sentiments only managed to have negative effects on his temperament; he became obstreperous, bad tempered, and arrogant, always ready to wax hyperbolic about his talents. His life was thus turned into a continuing succession of lies and sheer misery.<br /><br />He’s pitiful, yet humorous, reminiscent of Ignatius J. Reilly from A Confederacy of Dunces, another character whose ridiculous pride is the source of great comedic fodder. Like Ignatius, Ahmad is well read and refuses to believe that anyone is his intellectual equal, even though “all his readings were general; there was no specialization or depth involved.” When Ahmad visits the café in his new neighborhood, he meets a lawyer, Ahmad Rashid, who starts spouting off about Freud and Marx, who are unfamiliar to Ahmad Akif.<br /><br />He was no insanely angry with his companion, but was obviously unwilling to display his own ignorance. He shook his head as though he was well acquainted with the views of the two men.<br /><br />He’s a blustering fool, and we eagerly await his just deserts. But the unexpected happens: he falls in love. His 16-year-old neighbor, a girl with “honey-colored eyes,” is young enough to be his daughter. His room faces hers in the apartment complex, and each evening at sunset she honors him with respectful glances, which soon become smiles, “the kind his heart has craved for twenty long years.” This goes on for weeks, the diffident 40-year-old bachelor staring and smiling at Nawal, the beautiful, naïve, charming teenager. <br />	<br />	He realized, however, that it was not normal to settle for such exchanged glances and that he had to adopt a new approach. But could he do it? Was he actually capable of launching himself into life again just as he had managed to run away from it for all of twenty years?<br /><br />The answer to that question is put on hold when Rushdi, his brother, returns to Cairo. The younger Akif is everything his brother is not: handsome, confident, outgoing, intemperate and reckless. Rushdi’s room faces the same direction as his brother’s. And where Ahmad is indecisive, Rushdi is resolute. <br /><br />Where love was concerned, he had limitless self-confidence, based on one success after another. It was all founded on tremendous patience, an iron will that never gave up, and an innate suavity much assisted by artifice.<br /><br />Soon, Nawal is showering them both with appreciative glances, and our rooting interests are in question – on the one hand, there’s the self-sacrificing, delusional pedant who’s been loveless for twenty years; on the other, the charming, carousing cad who’s been living it up. Mahfouz could have made it easy to choose sides, but the brothers, alas, love each other, so our loyalties remain unsteady.<br /><br />Rushdi adored his elder brother because the latter had crafted him with his own two hands, nourished him with his spirit, and spent his own money on his younger brother’s upbringing.<br /><br />Ahmad, for his part, thinks Nawal is his last chance for happiness. But getting the girl is just the beginning of this tightly focused tale. Illness and death intercede, affecting both of their families, and concerns about the war (will the Germans invade?) are never far from mind. <br /><br />Unlike the books of The Cairo Trilogy, each of which covers several years and includes the point of views of many characters, Khan al-Khalili takes place in one year and includes the point of views of just Ahmad, Rushdi and Nawal. That’s more than enough, though, as Mahfouz explores the wants and needs of three people in love. In the end, at least one of them will be heartbroken, but will all be lost? <br /><br />	Life was dumb and cruel, like the dirt of the earth, and yet it could nurture hope.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3382911568453002789" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_217938811" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/217938811" />
    <a title="Elizabeth Periale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1420080-elizabeth-periale"><img alt="Elizabeth Periale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239211665p2/1420080.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/217938811?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth Periale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth Periale" href="/user/show/1420080-elizabeth-periale">Elizabeth Periale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780307742575" href="/book/show/12526296-khan-al-khalili">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer217938811" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14726130016627927762"><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://xoxoxoe.blogspot.com/2012/03/khan-al-khalili.html'>http://xoxoxoe.blogspot.com/2012/03/k...</a><br/><br/>"Nobel Prize-winning author (1988) Mahfouz paints a compelling picture of Cairo during the war — from an angle that many readers have probably never encountered before. The men that Ahmad spends time with at the Zahra Cafe are constantly debating politics and ideas. Some are sympathetic to Hitler and the Germans, some to the Russians, but most are not fond of the British, who occupied Egypt from the 1880s until the 1930s. But nothing, especially political </span>
  <span id="freeText14726130016627927762" style="display:none"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://xoxoxoe.blogspot.com/2012/03/khan-al-khalili.html">http://xoxoxoe.blogspot.com/2012/03/k...</a><br /><br />"Nobel Prize-winning author (1988) Mahfouz paints a compelling picture of Cairo during the war — from an angle that many readers have probably never encountered before. The men that Ahmad spends time with at the Zahra Cafe are constantly debating politics and ideas. Some are sympathetic to Hitler and the Germans, some to the Russians, but most are not fond of the British, who occupied Egypt from the 1880s until the 1930s. But nothing, especially political sympathies, are simple for Ahmad or his circle. Cairo is caught in the crossfire, and there are as many criticisms of the Germans as hopeful thinking that they will banish the Brits and end the war.<br /><br />Egyptian food and religious festivals, especially Ramadan, provide backdrop and texture for many of the emotional events in the novel. Soon the interpersonal tragedies of the Akifs far outweigh the fear and inconvenience of being rousted out of bed and having to go to a bomb shelter in the middle of the night. The war is always there in the background, but it is Ahmad's growth, the opening of his heart, that is the main focus of the novel. The war and other circumstances all join to drag Ahmad from his classical and familiar Arabic texts into a more modern, if irrevocably changed, world. Khan al-Khalili is a great introduction to the work of Naguib Mahfouz and the rich and complicated life of modern Egypt and Egyptians."</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2227345742" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2227345742" />
    <a title="Borys Musielak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25060512-borys-musielak"><img alt="Borys Musielak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1382736860p2/25060512.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2227345742?book_show_action=true">Dec 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Borys Musielak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Borys Musielak" href="/user/show/25060512-borys-musielak">Borys Musielak</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788364846274" href="/book/show/25388903-chan-al-chalili">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2227345742" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12015285496851703798">It's very similar in pace and substance to his previous book about Hamida and al-Midakk, but in this one there is more emphasis on the two main characters (the brothers) and less focus on the side characters (the other book was about everyone and no one). I liked both but neither comes close to Mahouz's later work (the trilogy or "the thief and the dogs").</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14060827287224939631" style="display:none">reading experience was badly marred by pedestrian translation - roger allen's afterword talks about the "poetic quality" of mahfouz's descriptions but the english versions are just clunky, like: "the way their fringes sparkled in the sunlight was eye-catching." maybe the original is like this and it's a stylistic clash on my part, but if not then <i>come on</i>.<br /><br />that aside i can certainly see why mahfouz got a nobel, even if it wasn't for this book - the characters here are unconventional leads, all of them flawed one way or another, yet you get to know them, understand them, and feel engaged, even though sympathy or empathy may not be a factor. it was an interesting read, and enjoyable outside of (what i hope was) the translation problem.</span>

<div id="review_3270312671" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3270312671" />
    <a title="Jerry Pogan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59682834-jerry-pogan"><img alt="Jerry Pogan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538604661p2/59682834.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3270312671?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jerry Pogan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jerry Pogan" href="/user/show/59682834-jerry-pogan">Jerry Pogan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789774161919" href="/book/show/4900242-khan-al-khalili">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3270312671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12407270839475603592">A brilliantly written family drama set in Cairo during WWII. The main protagonist is Ahmad Akif who has a delusional image of himself as a superior intellectual even though he has failed in his efforts to pursue that vocation and at forty years of age is still living with his parents. Added to that is a complete fear of woman so he is unable to form any type of relationship. With this as the backdrop Mahfouz weaved a marvelous tale involving Ahmad's younger brother and his parents. As good as th</span>
  <span id="freeText12407270839475603592" style="display:none">A brilliantly written family drama set in Cairo during WWII. The main protagonist is Ahmad Akif who has a delusional image of himself as a superior intellectual even though he has failed in his efforts to pursue that vocation and at forty years of age is still living with his parents. Added to that is a complete fear of woman so he is unable to form any type of relationship. With this as the backdrop Mahfouz weaved a marvelous tale involving Ahmad's younger brother and his parents. As good as the main story was, however, Mahfouz was able to insert a wonderful amount of detail in Egyptian political and religious thinking for this period of time. Perhaps my favorite Mahfouz book yet.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12407270839475603592" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('RThktROVEvX3OYCDmJ7sSjWj5fjpM6eQLMcputgw6+xkVjCp1hVHksL+WTlY+K0Fsb4vCWUtAA0pnzBZPuS/fg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JpM4xzqp8DwToaQkqEO4eZTRq7lDPhARHSdrppJ1disH/Wzb/ymlWyZmfZ5oJfk2EMxhSM8gt4wYf3JFdKEiuQ==')})
  }
//]]>

            if ($('bookCover_1914211').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });

      $('bookCover_1914211').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {

            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);

        });

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5498563451551930916">Naguib Mahfouz (Arabic author profile: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4273240._' rel='nofollow noopener'>نجيب محفوظ</a>) was an Egyptian writer who won the 1988 Nobel Prize for Literature. He published over 50 novels, over 350 short stories, dozens of movie scripts, and five plays over a 70-year career. Many of his works have been made into Egyptian and foreign films.</span>
  <span id="freeText5498563451551930916" style="display:none">Naguib Mahfouz (Arabic author profile: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4273240._" rel="nofollow noopener">نجيب محفوظ</a>) was an Egyptian writer who won the 1988 Nobel Prize for Literature. He published over 50 novels, over 350 short stories, dozens of movie scripts, and five plays over a 70-year career. Many of his works have been made into Egyptian and foreign films.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5498563451551930916" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
